## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: nonlinear oscillator dynamics
**Analysis**: This task models the acceleration of a nonlinear harmonic oscillator. The acceleration typically includes restoring force terms (linear and nonlinear in x), damping terms (in v), and possibly time-dependent driving forces.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Simple harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x (linear restoring force)
- Damped harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v (linear restoring + linear damping)
- Duffing oscillator (undriven): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (cubic nonlinear restoring force)
- Damped Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v (cubic restoring + damping)
- Driven damped oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * cos(c3 * t) (sinusoidal external drive)
- Driven Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (full Duffing equation)
- Asymmetric nonlinear oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**3 (quadratic + cubic nonlinearity)
- Van der Pol oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * (1 - c2 * x**2) * v (nonlinear damping)
- Nonlinear damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v * Abs(v) (quadratic velocity damping)
- Quintic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * x**5 (higher-order restoring force)
- Parametric oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x * cos(c1 * t) (time-modulated stiffness)
- Coupled position-velocity nonlinearity: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 * v + c2 * v (position-dependent damping)
- Softening/hardening spring with drive: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * sin(c4 * t + c5) (phase-shifted driving)
- Polynomial restoring + linear damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**3 + c3 * v (general low-order nonlinear oscillator)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Odd-power restoring forces (x, x³, x⁵) → symmetric double-well or hardening/softening potential
- Quadratic position terms (x²) in restoring force → asymmetric potential well, broken symmetry
- Velocity-dependent terms proportional to (1 - x²)*v → self-sustained oscillation (limit cycle behavior, Van der Pol type)
- Periodic time dependence via sin/cos(c*t) → external periodic driving force causing resonance or chaos
- Product terms x*v or x²*v → position-dependent damping or energy-dependent dissipation
- Cubic restoring force sign: positive c1 in x³ → hardening spring; negative → softening spring (Duffing classification)
- Absence of explicit time dependence → autonomous system, energy may be conserved or dissipated depending on v terms
- Rational terms like x/(1 + c*x²) → bounded nonlinear restoring force approaching constant for large displacement
- Mixed parity terms (both x² and x³) → oscillator with both asymmetry and nonlinear stiffness

The critical challenge is representing $xe^{-|x|}$ and $\sin(v)$.

**For $\sin(v)$**: The Taylor series is $v - v^3/6 + v^5/120 - ...$. No finite polynomial in $v$ can exactly equal $\sin(v)$. So candidates that only have polynomial terms in $v$ (like $v$, $v^3$) cannot match. Similarly, $v\cdot|v|$ is not $\sin(v)$.

**For $xe^{-|x|}$**: Note that $|x|$ makes this an even-in-sign function times $x$, so it's odd. The function $xe^{-|x|}$ cannot be represented by:
- Polynomials in $x$ (finite sum)
- $x\log(cx^2+1)$ — this is a different function
- $x/(cx^2+1)$ — rational function, different
- $xe^{cx^2}$ — this is $xe^{cx^2}$, not $xe^{-|x|}$. For $c < 0$, $e^{cx^2} \neq e^{-|x|}$ since $x^2 \neq |x|$ in general.
- $x^c$ power terms — different

None of these basis functions can exactly represent $xe^{-|x|}$ or $\sin(v)$.

Let me verify: Is $e^{-|x|} = e^{cx^2}$ for some $c$? No, because $|x| \neq cx^2$ for all $x$.

Is $\sin(v)$ representable as $av + bv^3$? No, this is only an approximation.

Is $\sin(v) = v\cdot|v|$ possible? No, $v|v| = v^2\text{sgn}(v)$, which is different from $\sin(v)$.

Since **no candidate** has both $xe^{-|x|}$ (or equivalently $xe^{-\text{Abs}(x)}$) and $\sin(v)$ as basis functions, none can exactly match the GT.

- No candidate contains $\sin(v)$ at all.
